        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942"><meta property="og:title" content="MnFe2O4@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/acs.nanolett.6b00942.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="MnFe2O4 nanodots (∼3.3 nm) homogeneously dispersed in porous nitrogen-doped carbon nanofibers (denoted as MFO@C) were prepared by a feasible electrospinning technique. Meanwhile, MFO@C with the character of flexible free-standing membrane was directly used as binder- and current collector-free anode for sodium-ion batteries, exhibiting high electrochemical performance with high-rate capability (305 mA h g–1 at 10000 mA g–1 in comparison of 504 mA h g–1 at 100 mA g–1) and ultralong cycling life (ca. 90% capacity retention after 4200 cycles). The Na-storage mechanism was systematically studied, revealing that MnFe2O4 is converted into metallic Mn and Fe after the first discharge (MnFe2O4 + 8Na+ + 8e– → Mn + 2Fe + 4Na2O) and then to MnO and Fe2O3 during the following charge (Mn + 2Fe + 4Na2O → MnO + Fe2O3 + 8Na+ + 8e–). The subsequent cycles occur through reversible redox reactions of MnO + Fe2O3 + 8Na+ + 8e– ↔ Mn + 2Fe + 4Na2O, of which the reduction/oxidation of MnO/Mn takes place at a lower potential than that of Fe2O3/Fe. Furthermore, a soft package sodium-ion full battery with MFO@C anode and Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C cathode was assembled, delivering a stable capacity of ∼400 mA h g–1 for MFO@C (with 100 cycles at 500 mA g–1) and a promising energy density of 77.8 Wh kg–1 for the whole battery. This is owing to the distinctive structure of very-fine MnFe2O4 nanodots embedded in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers, which effectively improves the utilization rate of active materials, facilitates the transportation of electrons and Na+ ions, and prevents the particle pulverization/agglomeration upon prolonged cycling."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/acs.nanolett.6b00942.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90f6e2797e3a775c-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2797e3a775c-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '02bff3b1-789e-40e0-8da7-13f06639d86f'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        <script async="" type="text/javascript" src="https://www.googletagservices.com/tag/js/gpt.js" rel="preconnect" nonce="90f6e2797e3a775c-SJC"></script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6e2797e3a775c-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

title="Download to MS-PowerPoint" class="figure-viewer_ms-p-link"><i class="icon-file-powerpoint-o"></i><span>Download to MS-PowerPoint</span></a><span class="cit-this-container"><a href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span class="articleHeaderDropzone5" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone5"><!-- Article Refresh Event Tracking -->
<script nonce="90f6e2797e3a775c-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "MnFe&lt;sub&gt;2&lt;/sub&gt;O&lt;sub&gt;4&lt;/sub&gt;@C Nanofibers as High-Performance Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

name="citation-copy-value" value="Nano Lett. 2016, 16, 5, 3321–3328"><span class="cite-this">Cite this: </span><span class="cit-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span> <span class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3321%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYongchang%2BLiu%252C%2BNing%2BZhang%252C%2BChuanming%2BYu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D5%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.6b00942%26title%3DMnFe2O4%2540C%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3328%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2016" data-id="article_rights_permissions">Request reuse permissions</a></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_header-metrics-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="article_header-metrics-dropzone"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-00942v_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanodots (∼3.3 nm) homogeneously dispersed in porous nitrogen-doped carbon nanofibers (denoted as MFO@C) were prepared by a feasible electrospinning technique. Meanwhile, MFO@C with the character of flexible free-standing membrane was directly used as binder- and current collector-free anode for sodium-ion batteries, exhibiting high electrochemical performance with high-rate capability (305 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> in comparison of 504 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and ultralong cycling life (ca. 90% capacity retention after 4200 cycles). The Na-storage mechanism was systematically studied, revealing that MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is converted into metallic Mn and Fe after the first discharge (MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> → Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O) and then to MnO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> during the following charge (Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O → MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup>). The subsequent cycles occur through reversible redox reactions of MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> ↔ Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O, of which the reduction/oxidation of MnO/Mn takes place at a lower potential than that of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>/Fe. Furthermore, a soft package sodium-ion full battery with MFO@C anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C cathode was assembled, delivering a stable capacity of ∼400 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> for MFO@C (with 100 cycles at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and a promising energy density of 77.8 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> for the whole battery. This is owing to the distinctive structure of very-fine MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanodots embedded in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers, which effectively improves the utilization rate of active materials, facilitates the transportation of electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> ions, and prevents the particle pulverization/agglomeration upon prolonged cycling.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D3321%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYongchang%2BLiu%252C%2BNing%2BZhang%252C%2BChuanming%2BYu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D16%26issueNum%3D5%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.6b00942%26title%3DMnFe2O4%2540C%2BNanofibers%2Bas%2BHigh-Performance%2BAnode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D3328%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2016" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2016 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291493&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanomaterials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon%20nanomaterials" class="article__tags__link">Carbon nanomaterials</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291009&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanofibers" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanofibers" class="article__tags__link">Nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Transition%20metals" class="article__tags__link">Transition metals</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Mnfe2o4+Nanodots&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_MnFe2O4%20nanodots">MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanodots</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Porous+N-doped+Carbon+Nanofibers&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_porous%20N-doped%20carbon%20nanofibers">porous N-doped carbon nanofibers</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Electrospinning&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_electrospinning">electrospinning</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Free-standing+Anode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_free-standing%20anode">free-standing anode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have recently attracted a great interest as a potential alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) because sodium resources are virtually inexhaustible and ubiquitous around the world. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1-4)</a> The working mechanism of SIBs is similar to that of LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> However, the larger and heavier Na<sup>+</sup> ion (radius 1.02 Å, mass 22.99 g mol<sup>–1</sup>) as compared to Li<sup>+</sup> ion (radius 0.59 Å, mass 6.94 g mol<sup>–1</sup>) makes it difficult to identify appropriate electrode materials with the capability of fast and stable sodium-ion insertion/extraction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6 ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6, 7)</a> As an effective strategy, transition-metal oxides with conversion reactions have been adopted. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8-10)</a> Alcantara et al. used NiCo<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> spinel oxide as anode material of SIBs, demonstrating a reversible capacity of ∼200 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> In comparison, spinel MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with natural abundance, low cost, nontoxic nature, and high electrochemical activity is attractive in LIBs, supercapacitors, and metal-air batteries, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13 ref14 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 12 to 16">(12-16)</a> but its Na-storage behavior is scarcely reported.</p><p>MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is usually prepared through coprecipitation, reflux, or solvothermal process. Zhang’s group first obtained MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanocrystals by coprecipitation of FeCl<sub>3</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and MnCl<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O in 1998. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> Sun and colleagues also prepared MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles by refluxing of Fe(acac)<sub>3</sub> and Mn(acac)<sub>2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/graphene hybrid was then synthesized via a solvothermal method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> In comparison, electrospinning technique has been employed to prepare nanostructured electrode materials such as Sn, Sb, and MoS<sub>2</sub> embedded in carbon nanofibers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23" aria-label="reference 20 to 23">(20-23)</a> Such tailored nanocomposites significantly improved the Na-storage performance because ultrasmall grains (especially within 10 nm) could endure the mechanical stress caused by large volume fluctuation during sodiation/desodiation. Meanwhile, one-dimensional carbon nanofibers would effectively enhance the electronic/ionic transport. Since transition-metal oxides generally face the problems of large volume expansion and poor conductivity in SIBs, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 24 to 26">(24-26)</a> and furthermore, the synthesis of ultrasmall MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (&lt;10 nm) that are dispersed in carbon nanofibers has not yet been developed. It is of great interest to apply electrospinning to design such nanostructured MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C as the anode of Na-ion batteries.</p><p>Herein, we report the fabrication of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanodots (with typical size of ∼3.3 nm) finely encapsulated in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers (denoted as MFO@C) via an electrospinning approach and their anode application for SIBs. The as-prepared MFO@C nanofibers, which are weaved into flexible free-standing membrane, can be directly used as binder- and current collector-free electrode, demonstrating excellent electrochemical performance with high-rate capability (305 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and ultralong cycle life (ca. 90% capacity retention after 4200 cycles). It is demonstrated that the Na-storage mechanism occurs through the reaction of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> → Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O during the first discharge and then to MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> from Mn + Fe in the first charge; While it is MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> ↔ Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O during the following cycles. Moreover, a soft package sodium-ion full battery with MFO@C anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C cathode delivers an energy density of 77.8 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> for the whole battery, showing potential for large-scale application.</p><p>The preparation procedure and experimental optimization for MFO@C nanofibers are provided in the Experimental Section and Figure S1–S3 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a shows the Rietveld refined X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of MFO@C nanofibers. All the diffraction peaks can be indexed to the cubic spinel-type MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (JCPDS card No. 10-0319, space group: <i>Fd</i>3̅<i>m</i>, <i>a</i> = 8.50 Å). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> No other peaks are observed, implying the high purity of the product. Based on the refinement result, the unit cell structure is constructed by MnO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra and FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b). For comparison, MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C nanofibers with lower/higher MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> content (denoted as L-MFO@C/H-MFO@C) were also fabricated by altering the mass ratio of metal salts and carbon source. The XRD patterns of L-MFO@C and H-MFO@C are given in Figure S4a (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) in comparison with MFO@C, which are in accordance with cubic MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. No intensive peak belonging to carbon is observed, demonstrating the amorphous nature of carbon. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> This is supported by Raman spectra of the three MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C samples (Figure S4b, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The two broad peaks at 1342 and 1587 cm<sup>–1</sup> are assigned to the defect-induced (D) mode and graphenic-induced (G) mode of amorphous carbon, respectively. Moreover, the <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> ratio is in the range of 1.15–1.18, indicating that there are abundant defects and vacancies in carbon nanofibers. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> This not only favors the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions but also provides more intercalation sites for Na-storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> The other peak at 608 cm<sup>–1</sup> is attributed to the spinel MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c presents the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image of MFO@C nanofibers. The nanofibers are smooth and continuous with an average diameter of 180 nm, which interlink into a three-dimensional (3D) network. Energy dispersive spectrocopy (EDS) mapping images (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d) reveal uniform distributions of Mn, Fe, O, and N along the carbon nanofibers. Transmission electron microscopy (TEM, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>e) and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>f) images of MFO@C further verify that all the MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (black dots) are homogeneously encapsulated in the fibrous carbon matrix (gray color). It is worthwhile to emphasize that the MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> grains are ultrasmall with an average size of 3.3 nm (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>g, by measuring 100 particles using Nano-Measure software). As far as we know, these are the smallest MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles ever reported. Based on XRD data, the crystalline size of the MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> grains calculated using Debye–Scherrer equation is about 11 nm (Table S1, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), which is larger than the TEM observation result (∼3.3 nm). This is possibly due to the local agglomeration of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> Meanwhile, the instrument parameters (such as operating power, energy resolution, goniometer precision, and slit width) would also affect the calculation result. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The clear lattice fringes with interplanar distances of 0.30 and 0.21 nm correspond to the (220) and (400) planes of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with an angle of 45° between the two planes (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>h), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> which is in good agreement with the standard value.</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-00942v_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Structural and morphological analyses of the as-prepared MFO@C nanofibers: (a) Rietveld refined XRD pattern with experimental data (red dots), calculated curves (cyan line), position of allowed Bragg reflections (green vertical bars), and difference profile (blue line). (b) Crystal structure with MnO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra (purple) and FeO<sub>6</sub> octahedra (green). (c) SEM and (d) EDS mapping images. (e) TEM and (f) HRTEM images. (g) MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> particle size distribution diagram and (h) lattice fringes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>For comparison, the SEM and TEM images of L-MFO@C and H-MFO@C are displayed in Figure S5 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). L-MFO@C also exists in a reticular morphology interconnected by smooth nanofibers, while many particles are exposed on the surface of H-MFO@C nanofibers. The particle size of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in L-MFO@C is ∼3.2 nm, which can not be further reduced with its content decreasing. However, the particle size of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> grows dramatically to ∼36.5 nm in H-MFO@C. The compositions of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C are determined by elemental analysis as summarized in Table S2 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The contents of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in L-MFO@C, MFO@C, and H-MFO@C are 56.1, 66.8, and 72.9 wt %, respectively. This is also demonstrated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Figure S6 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>).</p><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a presents the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm of MFO@C nanofibers, exhibiting a typical type-IV behavior with a distinct hysteresis loop at relative pressure (<i>P</i>/<i>P</i><sub>0</sub>) ranging from 0.45 to 1. This implies that the MFO@C nanofibers contain a large number of mesopores. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref21" aria-label="reference 9 and 21">(9, 21)</a> The pore size is mainly distributed between 3 and 5 nm (inset). According to the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) method, MFO@C nanofibers possess a large surface area of 239.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was used to gain insight into the chemical compositions and valence of MFO@C. No peaks of elements other than Mn, Fe, O, C, and N are observed in the survey spectrum (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) with the atomic ratio of Mn/Fe/O = 1:2:4. Mn 2p spectrum (Figure S8a in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) shows two characteristic peaks at 641.8 eV (Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub>) and 653.5 eV (Mn 2p<sub>1/2</sub>), indicating the oxidation state Mn<sup>2+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Two major peaks in Fe 2p spectrum (Figure S8b in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) at 711.2 and 724.8 eV are ascribed to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Fe<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The high-resolution N 1s spectrum of MFO@C in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c shows four fitted peaks centered at 398.4, 399.7, 401.0, and 402.1 eV, which are attributed to pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, graphitic N, and oxidized N, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> The area percentages of the above fitted peaks are 16.7%, 51.8%, 17.5%, and 14%, successively, representing the content percentages of the corresponding four N species (inset table below <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c,d). The high-resolution C 1s spectrum in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d further demonstrates the formation of C–N and C═N bonds. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e schematically illustrates the nitrogen configurations. First, the doped N can contribute more electrons to the π-conjugated system of carbon, thus enhancing the electronic conductivity of carbon nanofibers. Second, pyridinic N and pyrrolic N can create some defects among carbon nanofibers, providing more diffusion channels and active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> This is proved by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS, Figure S9 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The resistance of MFO@C is much lower than that of the N-free sample and H-MFO@C. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of MFO@C is also calculated to be the largest (Table S3 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Therefore, the uniform dispersion of ultrasmall MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in N-doped carbon nanofibers could facilitate the electrons transfer and Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion kinetics.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-00942v_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Porosity and N-doped character of MFO@C nanofibers: (a) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm and pore size distribution curve (inset). (b) Survey XPS spectrum. (c) N 1s and (d) C 1s high-resolution XPS spectra (inset table shows the content percentages of the corresponding four N types). (e) Schematic configurations of pyridinic N, pyrrolic N, and graphitic N in MFO@C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The as-prepared MFO@C fiber paper with a great flexibility could be cut into free-standing electrodes for SIBs (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). Neither mechanical milling nor slurry casting in traditional processing for battery production was needed. Specifically, the MFO@C membrane was punched out to appropriate size and directly assembled in coin-type cells that employ sodium foil as the counter/reference electrode and 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate (PC) solution with 5 vol % fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as the electrolyte. The optimized mass of self-supported working electrode is ∼2.0 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>, which can be controlled by altering the electrospinning time. All the specific capacity values in this letter are based on the total mass of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C composites. The electrochemical reactivity of MFO@C was evaluated by cyclic voltammograms (CVs, <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a). The first cathodic scan shows a strong peak at 0.56 V, which is associated with the formation of solid-electrolyte interface (SEI) film and the reduction of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to metallic Mn and Fe, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> as expressed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a>. The broad anodic peak at 1.65 V is ascribed to the oxidation of Mn and Fe to MnO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>). Such a result is confirmed by the ex situ XRD patterns of MFO@C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b, in which parafilm is used to cover the electrode from exposure to air during XRD measurement) after the first full sodiation (discharge to 0.01 V) and desodiation (charge to 3.0 V) processes.<span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq1"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m001.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq2"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m002.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></span></p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-00942v_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Na-storage mechanism of MFO@C nanofibers: (a) Cyclic voltammetry curves scanned at a rate of 0.05 mV s<sup>–1</sup> in the voltage window of 0.01–3.0 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. (b) XRD patterns and (c) HRTEM images of MFO@C electrode materials at selected charged/discharged states (current density = 20 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). (d) Schematic illustration for the mechanism of MFO@C nanofibers during repeated sodiation/desodiation in the first cycle and the subsequent cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The subsequent sweeps are mainly characterized by two pairs of redox peaks. The one at 0.4/1.26 V corresponds to the reduction/oxidation of MnO/Mn (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>), <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> the other one at 0.8/1.7 V corresponds to the reduction/oxidation of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>/Fe (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="4">4</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> This is also demonstrated by ex situ XRD results. In the second cycle, when the electrode is discharged to 0.6 V, the peaks of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> are replaced by metallic Fe, while the peaks of MnO are still observed; when the electrode is fully discharged to 0.01 V, the reaction product is mixture of Mn and Fe. During the charge process, metallic Mn is oxidized to MnO, while peaks of Fe still exist at the charge to 1.5 V; MnO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> reappear after fully charged to 3.0 V. Similarly, ex situ Raman spectra (Figure S10 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) also confirm that MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is converted into Mn and Fe after the first discharge and then to MnO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> during the following charge. While the subsequent cycles occur through reversible redox reactions of MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> to Mn + Fe (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eq 5">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a>), of which Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> is first reduced to Fe and then MnO to Mn during the discharge, Mn is first oxidized to MnO and then Fe to Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> during the charge. In addition, a cathodic peak near 0.02 V and a small counterpart anodic peak at 0.12 V are observed in the CV curves, resembling the cycle voltammetry behavior of sodium insertion/extraction in carbon nanofibers (Figure S11 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> It is noted that from the second cycle onward, CV curves mostly overlap, indicating the good reversibility of the electrochemical reactions described in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="eqs 5">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq5" aria-label="5">5</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="6">6</a>. Therefore, the theoretical capacity of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with 8-electron conversion reaction is calculated to be 926 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> using the formula in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq7" aria-label="eq 7">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq7" aria-label="7">7</a>, where <i>F</i> is Faraday constant, <i>n</i> is the electron transfer number, and <i>M</i><sub><i>w</i></sub> is the molecular weight of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq3"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m003.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m004.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m005.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq6"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m006.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula" id="eq7"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/nl-2016-00942v_m007.gif" alt=""><span class="labelSpan">(7)</span></span></p><p>HRTEM images of MFO@C electrode materials at the same charged/discharged states as ex situ XRD further manifest the above reaction mechanism (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c). More impressively, it is discovered that the reaction products are surrounded by each other intimately, rather than separated. In view of the different redox potentials of MnO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> with Na<sup>+</sup> ions to Mn + Fe + Na<sub>2</sub>O, when one metal oxide undergoes sodiation/desodiation, the other counterpart could act as an inert buffer matrix around it, and this alleviates the pulverization or aggregation caused by volume change (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). This self-buffering effect is taking the role for enhancing the cyclic stability of MFO@C electrode.</p><p><a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the rate capability and cycling performance of the as-synthesized MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>@C nanofibers. During the initial 20 cycles, the MFO@C electrode delivers a reversible capacity of 504 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Then, the rate is gradually increased to 200, 500, 1000, 2000, 5000, and 10000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> with ten cycles staying at each rate. The corresponding capacities can reach 485, 449, 413, 391, 340, and 305 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The ultrasmall MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanodots with large specific area are accessible to the electrolyte and easy to be effectively utilized for Na-storage. Meanwhile, the high porosity and high electric conductivity of the whole fibrous network would fasten the transportation of electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> ions, thus rendering the high-rate capability. More importantly, when the current density is brought back to 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the reversible capacity swiftly recovers to 501 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, showing a strong tolerance for the rapid Na ion insertion/extraction. The cell is further tested at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> up to 300 cycles, and the capacity maintains stable at ∼445 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> with negligible fading. By contrast, the specific capacity of L-MFO@C is much lower due to its limited MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> content. While for H-MFO@C electrode, it delivers a relatively high initial reversible capacity of 510 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> but shows inferior rate capability and poor cycling performance (only 150 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and 260 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 250 cycles). In addition, the electrochemical properties of carbon-wrapped manganese oxide and iron oxide mixture are also investigated (details see Figure S12 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Since the mixability of the two metal oxides is not as intimate as that of the reaction products in situ coming from MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, its cycling performance is not comparable to that of MFO@C. The TEM image and electrochemical performance of carbon nanofibers are also provided in Figure S12, displaying a great cyclic stability with the capacity of 220 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, the addition of FEC in electrolyte can enhance the structural stability of SEI film, strengthen the electrode architecture, and suppress the side reactions between electrode and electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 37 and 38">(37, 38)</a> This is favorable for reducing the irreversible capacity and improving the cycling stability. The electrochemical performance of MFO@C in FEC-free NaClO<sub>4</sub>/PC electrolyte is also presented in Figure S13 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) to reflect the difference. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b displays the representative galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of MFO@C electrode at various current densities, further demonstrating the high-rate capability and high capacity retention. Furthermore, the initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) is calculated to be 70.3%. Generally, this CE is high, suggesting that the encapsulation of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles in the porous carbon matrix can significantly alleviate detrimental reactions between MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and electrolyte. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The capacity loss in the first cycle is mainly attributed to the formation of SEI film. <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig4" aria-label="Figure 4">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c shows the long-term cycling performance of MFO@C electrode at 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. The reversible capacity can reach 401 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> and retain ∼360 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (∼90% retention) after 4200 cycles even at such a high rate. Remarkably, the CE approaches 100% (&gt;99.6%), which is beneficial for the ultralong cycle life. The high CE and high stability over long cycling also indicate that the generated Mn and Fe intermediates are stable with the electrolyte. The exceptional high-rate capability and cycling stability of MFO@C are summarized in Table S4 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>), which compare favorably with those previously reported for Mn/Fe-based oxides as anodes of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8 ref9 ref24 ref34 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref24 ref34 ref39" aria-label="reference 8, 9, 24, 34 and 39">(8, 9, 24, 34, 39)</a> More excitingly, this is also a very outstanding performance among all the hot-studied Na-storage anodes, including P/C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref37" aria-label="reference 5 and 37">(5, 37)</a> Sn/C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Sb/C, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> and MoS<sub>2</sub>/C. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a></p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-00942v_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties: (a) Rate capability and cycling performance of MFO@C, L-MFO@C, and H-MFO@C electrodes in the potential window of 0.01–3.0 V. (b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of MFO@C at different rates. (c) Long-term cycling stability of MFO@C electrode at a current density of 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The morphological and structural changes of MFO@C electrode after high-rate and long-term cycling were examined using SEM and TEM. As revealed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b, MFO@C after 300 cycles still retains its original appearance and structure with homogeneous distribution. The insert digital image in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a verifies that the electrode still maintains its integrity and flexibility after 300 cycles. In contrast, the large particles of H-MFO@C could not endure the deformation stress generated over repeated sodiation/desodiation, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref28" aria-label="reference 21 and 28">(21, 28)</a> which have collapsed and aggregated to some extent after 250 cycles (Figure S14 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). This should account for the rapid capacity decay of H-MFO@C.</p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/medium/nl-2016-00942v_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) SEM image and digital photo (inset) of the MFO@C electrode charged at 3.0 V after 300 cycles. (b) TEM and EDS mapping images of a typical MFO@C nanofiber taken from the electrode charged at 3.0 V after 300 cycles. (c) Charge/discharge curves and (d) cycling performance of Al-plastic film soft package Na-ion full battery with MFO@C–Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C (inset in c) tested in the voltage range of 1.0–3.5 V at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/asset/images/large/nl-2016-00942v_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The excellent performance of MFO@C/Na half cell encourages us to further evaluate its practicability in a Na-ion full cell. The MFO@C self-supported anode was paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C cathode that delivers a stable capacity of ∼100 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> in a half cell (Figure S15 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). An Al-plastic film soft package battery was assembled (inset of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c; for fabrication details see Experimental Section in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). The active material weight ratio of anode to cathode was carefully balanced and set at 1:5, following the industrial standard. When tested between 1.0 and 3.5 V, the MFO@C–Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C full battery was first activated at a relatively low current density of 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup> for 3 cycles (Figure S16 in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Afterward, it exhibits a discharge capacity of 406 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> based on the mass of anodic MFO@C with an average output voltage of ∼2.3 V at 500 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). This corresponds to an energy density of 77.8 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> based on the total mass of the battery (6.72 g). The freedom of either binder or current collector can significantly reduce the inactive weight of battery, thus improving the energy density. After 100 cycles, the discharge capacity remains ∼392 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, showing a high capacity retention (96.5%) with high CE of ∼99% (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig5" aria-label="Figure 5">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d). This indicates that MFO@C nanofibers have potential for practical applications.</p><p class="last">In summary, MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanodots (∼3.3 nm) were encapsulated in porous N-doped carbon nanofibers via a feasible electrospinning method. The as-prepared MFO@C nanofibers with flexible free-standing membrane were directly used as binder- and current collector-free anode for SIBs, demonstrating exceptional high-rate capability (305 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at 10000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and ultralong cycling life (ca. 90% capacity retention over 4200 cycles). The reaction mechanism of MnFe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> → Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O is taking place in the first discharge and Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O → MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> in the first charge. While, it is MnO + Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 8Na<sup>+</sup> + 8e<sup>–</sup> ↔ Mn + 2Fe + 4Na<sub>2</sub>O in the subsequent cycles. Additionally, the soft package Na-ion full battery with MFO@C anode and Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>2</sub>F<sub>3</sub>/C cathode displayed a promising energy density of 77.8 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. As the anode of sodium-ion batteries, this tailored MFO@C nanostructure has shown prominent advantages including the uniform dispersion of ultrasmall nanoparticles in carbon matrix with high electrochemical activity, the facilitation of electrons and Na<sup>+</sup> ions transportation in 3D conductive network interlinked by porous N-doped carbon nanofibers, and the depression of pulverization and aggregation of the active materials caused by volume change.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942" class="ext-link">10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed experimental procedures; additional materials, electrodes, and batteries characterization (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942/suppl_file/nl6b00942_si_001.pdf">nl6b00942_si_001.pdf (1.79 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.6b00942" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">
